The maximum number of integer points on a line within that triangle is achieved by lines that are parallel to the axes or to the line x+y=0, because those are the directions where the line can intersect the triangle in a "long" segment. Given a line L with equation y = mx + c, where m is rational (maybe slope). The integer points on L are those with x integer and y integer. If m is rational, say m = p/q in lowest terms, then y = (p/q)x + c. For y to be integer for integer x, we need c to be rational with denominator q, and x such that (p/q)x is integer modulo denominator. Usually, if c is rational and m is rational, then the line contains integer points for infinitely many x, but the spacing is q. So the set of integer points on L is an arithmetic progression with difference q (in x). So the number of integer points within a finite region depends on the range of x. For a triangle of size n, the maximum number of integer points on a line (with rational slope) that can be covered is roughly n / (something). For m=0 (horizontal) it's n. For m=∞ (vertical) it's n. For m=-1, it's n. For m=1, it's about n/2. For m=2, it's about n/3. So indeed, the maximum coverage per line among sunny lines (excluding those three families) might be less than n, but can we still get n points? Let's test a line with slope 1/2 (m = 1/2). We can try to design a configuration with some sunny lines covering points that are not easily covered by non-sunny lines. But we need to determine all k. Let's analyze generally.

We have n lines. Let S be the set of sunny lines, size k. The rest are non-sunny. Non-sunny lines are either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We can choose them arbitrarily as long as they are distinct. The covering requirement: For every point (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1, there exists a line among the n lines passing through it.

We need to characterize possible k for given n≥3.

We have two extremes: k=0 (all non-sunny) is easy: use all horizontal lines y=1..n (or all vertical lines x=1..n) or some combination. So k=0 possible.

What about k=n? That's all sunny. We suspect it's impossible for n≥3. We could cover (1,1) with y=x, (3,1) with something else. But then we have 2 sunny lines, we need to cover 4 points. It seems unlikely. So maybe k=2 is impossible for n=3.

But we already found a configuration with 3 sunny lines covering all points. So k=n works. For n=3, possible k are 0,1,3? Let's check k=2. Could we have 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny covering all points? The total points 6, with 2 sunny lines each covering at most 2 points (max). That's at most 4 points covered by sunny lines. However, we need to see if there exists a set of 3 lines with exactly 2 sunny that covers all points. Let's attempt a systematic search. The non-sunny line could be any of the three types: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We need to cover all points.

What about using a different non-sunny line like x + y = 4 (slope -1) covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. Remaining points: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's 3 points. Again, any sunny line covering two of them would be non-sunny. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points covered. So fails.

Thus for n=3, k=2 is impossible. So possible k are 0,1,3. That's interesting: k=n is possible. For n=4, we have 10 points. With 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. The non-sunny line can cover up to 4 points. Sunny lines each cover up to 2 points. We have 10 points. The non-sunny lines can be any of the three families. The maximum coverage per line is 4. What about using a horizontal line y=2 and a vertical line x=2? That's two non-sunny lines. y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) (3 points). x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) (3 points). Thus it seems k=2 might be impossible for n=4. But we need a rigorous proof for general n. The problem asks to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny satisfying covering condition. So we need to find, for a given n≥3, which k are possible. We've seen that k=0 is possible. For n=3, k=1 and k=3 are possible, but k=2 is not. For n=4, k=0,3 (and maybe 1,2?) We constructed k=3 works. Let's test k=2 for n=4 more systematically with other choices of non-sunny lines. Could we have a configuration with 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines such that the union covers all 10 points? The two non-sunny lines could be chosen to cover many points. The maximum distinct coverage by two non-sunny lines is 8 (if they are disjoint and each cover 4 points). But can we get 8? For example, take horizontal line y=1 covering 4 points, and horizontal line y=2 covering 3 points, but they are both horizontal (non-sunny). That's 2 non-sunny lines, covering 4+3=7 points (overlap at (1,2)? Actually y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). No overlap. So distinct coverage 7. The remaining points are (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) maybe? Let's list all points:

(1,1) y=1
(1,2) y=2
(1,3) not covered
(1,4) not covered

(2,3) not covered
(3,1) y=1
(3,2) y=2
(4,1) y=1

So uncovered: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2). That's 4 points. With 2 sunny lines, each covering at most 2 points, we could cover all 4 if we can pair them appropriately with sunny lines. Are there sunny lines covering pairs among these 4? The points: A=(1,3), B=(1,4), C=(2,3), D=(3,2). Check pairs:

- A(1,3) and C(2,3): horizontal (y=3) → not sunny.
- A and D(3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2 → sunny.
- B(1,4) and C(2,3): slope (3-4)/(2-1) = -1 → not sunny.
- B and D(3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- C(2,3) and D(3,2): slope -1 → not sunny.
- A and B: vertical → not sunny.
- C and D: slope -1.
- A and C: horizontal.
- B and D: slope -1.
- A and D: sunny.
- B and C: not sunny.

So only pair with sunny line is (A,D) (1,3)-(3,2) with slope -1/2. That's one sunny line covering 2 points. The other pair (B,C) is not sunny, so cannot be used. So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 of those points (if we pair (A,D) together). Then we need to cover B and C as well. They could be covered by a sunny line that passes through them, but that line is not sunny, so cannot be a sunny line. Could we cover B with one sunny line and C with another sunny line, but that would require two sunny lines, but we have only 2 sunny lines total. If we use one sunny line for (A,D), then we have one sunny line left. It can cover at most one of B or C, leaving the other uncovered. So fails. Could we instead use a sunny line that covers (B,C) but that's not sunny. So no.

Thus k=2 fails for n=4. So possible k are 0 and n-1? But we also have n=3, n-1=2, but we found k=2 impossible. For n=3, n-1=2 fails, but k=3 works. So the pattern is not simply n-1. For n=3, k=3 works. For n=4, k=3 works. For n=5, maybe k=4 works? But we need to determine all k for general n. Let's try to compute maximum coverage per sunny line for n. For a line L that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, the maximum number of integer points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 that lie on L is something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1? Actually we need to find the maximum possible size of intersection of such a line with P. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's denote P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. We need to find, for a line L (any real line), the maximum number of points from P_n that lie on L. Then we can bound the covering capability.

For n≥3, we can try to determine the maximum possible number of points from P_n on a line that is not parallel to axes or x+y=0. Since we are looking for possible k, we can consider constructing configurations with many sunny lines. The constraints might be that the number of sunny lines k must be at most n-2? Or at most something like n - ceil((n+1)/2)? But we need to analyze.

Observation: The set P_n is a right triangle of lattice points. Lines that are not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 can have at most something like n points? Actually we saw for n=4, max is 2. For n=3, max is 2. For n=5, let's compute. P_5: a,b≥1, a+b≤6. Points: a=1..5, b=1..5 but with a+b≤6. That's triangular number 15. Let's compute max points on a sunny line. The maximum per line among all lines (including non-sunny) is n (if we take a horizontal line y=c, with c from 1 to n, it covers (n+1-c) points, which is ≤ n, but when c=1, covers n points; when c=n, covers 1 point). So the maximum is n. For sunny lines, they cannot be horizontal/vertical/slope -1. But maybe they can still cover n points if they are, say, lines with slope 0? That's horizontal, not sunny. Slope ∞ vertical, not sunny. Slope -1 not sunny. So those are excluded. So we need to find the maximum coverage among lines with slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

Consider a line with slope m. The integer points on it are those with x integer and y integer, which occur if m is rational and intercept appropriate, else at most one point. So to maximize coverage, we want m rational with small denominator, and we want the line to intersect P_n in as many lattice points as possible. The worst case (for covering) is when the line is such that many points lie on it. The line y = x + c has slope 1. The number of points from P_n on it is the number of a ≥ 1 such that a + (a + c) ≤ n+1 → 2a ≤ n+1 - c → a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/2). So max when c is as small as possible, but c must be integer if we want integer points? Actually we can choose c to be any real number, but to get integer points, we need that the line passes through integer points. If c is not integer, then for integer a, y = a + c is not integer (unless c is integer). So to have many integer points, we need c to be integer. For slope 1, the line y = x + c with c integer will contain integer points (a, a+c) for all integer a. Then the number of such points within P_n is as derived, max when c is small (like 0) gives a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So max count = ceil((n+1)/2)? Actually if n is odd, (n+1)/2 is integer. For n=3, (n+1)/2 = 2 → 2 points. For n=4, (n+1)/2 = 2.5 → floor = 2 → 2 points. So max is floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2.

Now consider a line with slope 2 (y = 2x + c). For integer c, points are (a, 2a + c) for integer a. Condition a + (2a + c) = 3a + c ≤ n+1 → a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/3). Max when c is small, e.g., c = 0 → a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). That's about n/3. So smaller.

Thus the maximum possible number of points from P_n on a line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 appears to be achieved by slope 1 (or slope -1 but -1 is not sunny). So the maximum is floor((n+1)/2). But we also need to consider lines with slope like 1/2? That's also rational, denominator 2. For slope 1/2, points occur when x is even. The line y = (1/2)x + c. For integer c, points (2t, t + c) maybe. Let's analyze: For a line with rational slope p/q in lowest terms, integer points exist when x is a multiple of q. Then the number of integer points within a finite interval is roughly (range of x)/q. For slope 1/2, q=2, so about n/2 points. That could be similar to slope 1. But also we need to consider that the line might not be able to contain many points if the intercept is chosen appropriately to have integer points. For slope 1/2, if we take c = 0, then points (2,1),(4,2),(6,3)... So the count is floor(n/2). For n=4, floor(4/2)=2. That's also 2. For n=3, floor(3/2)=1. So slope 1/2 gives fewer. So slope 1 gives larger coverage.

Now consider slope -1/2 (which is sunny). That's similar to slope 1/2 but negative. It will also have about n/2 points if the intercept is chosen appropriately. But for n=4, we saw that line through (1,3)-(3,2) (slope -1/2) covers two points. That's n/2? For n=4, n/2=2. So indeed that's also 2. So the maximum coverage among sunny lines might be floor((n+1)/2) or maybe ceil((n+1)/2)? Let's compute for n=4, floor((n+1)/2) = floor(2.5)=2. That's achieved by slope 1 (y=x) with c=0 gives 2 points. So that's the maximum. For n=3, floor((n+1)/2)=2. Achieved by slope 1 with c=0. For n=5, floor((n+1)/2)=3. Slope 1 with c=0 gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) → 3 points. That's within P_5 (a+b ≤6). (3,3) sum=6, ok. So max is 3. Could a sunny line with slope 1/2 cover 3 points? For n=5, slope 1/2 gives points (2,1),(4,2),(6,3) but (6,3) a+b=9>6, so only (2,1) and (4,2) are in P_5, that's 2 points. So less.

Thus we have a bound: For a sunny line L, |P_n ∩ L| ≤ floor((n+1)/2). Let's denote M_n = floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, M=2; n=4, M=2; n=5, M=3; etc.

Now, we have n lines total. Let S be the set of sunny lines (size k). Then the union of all lines covers P_n. The sunny lines contribute at most k * M_n points (counting multiplicities), but distinct points are at most that. However, some points might be covered by non-sunny lines as well, so the total number of distinct points covered by all lines is at most (n - k) * (max coverage per non-sunny line) + k * M_n, but non-sunny lines can cover up to n points (if horizontal/vertical/slope -1). However, we need to cover all |P_n| = n(n+1)/2 points. So we need to see if there is a feasible configuration for given k. It might be that for large k, it's impossible because the sunny lines cannot cover enough points, but the non-sunny lines are limited in number (n - k). So we can derive an inequality.

Let L_non be the set of non-sunny lines (size n - k). Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (achieved by e.g., y=1, which covers n points). Actually does a line parallel to x-axis (horizontal) cover at most n points? For a horizontal line y = c, with c integer from 1 to n, the points with b = c and a ≥ 1, a ≤ n+1 - c. So number of points = n+1 - c, which is ≤ n. For c=1, it's n. So max = n. Similarly vertical lines x = c cover n+1 - c points, max n. Slope -1 lines x+y = c cover c-1 points, max n (when c = n+1). So indeed, each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So the total distinct points covered by all non-sunny lines is at most (n - k) * n. However, they may overlap, so the union size ≤ (n - k) * n.

Now the sunny lines cover at most k * M_n points (distinct). So the union of all lines is at most (n - k) * n + k * M_n. We need this to be at least |P_n| = n(n+1)/2. Thus a necessary condition is:

(n - k) * n + k * M_n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

We can compute M_n = floor((n+1)/2). Let's denote:

If n is even, say n = 2m. Then n+1 = 2m+1, floor((n+1)/2) = floor((2m+1)/2) = m. So M_n = m = n/2. If n is odd, n = 2m+1. Then n+1 = 2m+2, floor((n+1)/2) = m+1 = (n+1)/2. So M_n = (n+1)/2.

Now compute LHS: (n - k) * n + k * M_n = n^2 - nk + k M_n = n^2 + k (M_n - n).

Since M_n ≤ n/2 for even n, M_n - n is negative. For odd n, M_n = (n+1)/2, so M_n - n = (n+1)/2 - n = (n+1 - 2n)/2 = (1 - n)/2, which is negative. So LHS is decreasing in k, because as k increases, we replace non-sunny lines (which can cover up to n points) with sunny lines (which can cover at most M_n points). Since we have a fixed total number of lines n, the maximum coverage per line among non-sunny is n, among sunny is M_n. So to maximize coverage, we want as many non-sunny lines as possible. So if k is large, LHS becomes smaller. So the necessary condition is an upper bound on k.

Let's compute:

Case n even: n = 2m. Then M_n = m = n/2. So LHS = (n - k) * n + k * (n/2) = n^2 - nk + (nk/2) = n^2 - (nk/2). So LHS = n^2 - (n k)/2.

We need LHS ≥ n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So:

n^2 - (n k)/2 ≥ (n^2 + n)/2.

Multiply by 2: 2n^2 - nk ≥ n^2 + n → 2n^2 - nk - n^2 - n ≥ 0 → n^2 - nk - n ≥ 0 → n (n - k - 1) ≥ 0 → n - k - 1 ≥ 0 → k ≤ n - 1.

So necessary condition: k ≤ n - 1.

But we also have k ≤ n obviously. So this says k ≤ n - 1. That is, if n is even, k cannot be n. That's consistent with our earlier observation that k=n is impossible for n≥4 (n even). For n=3 (odd), we found k=n=3 is possible. So the even case gives a bound that k ≤ n-1. That's a necessary condition, but we need to determine whether all k ≤ n-1 are possible? For n even, we have k=0 possible, k=1 possible? Let's test n=4, we constructed k=3 (which is n-1) and k=0. What about k=1? Now, we also have n=3, k=2 impossible. So the pattern is not simply k ≤ n-1, because for n=3, k=n-1=2 fails. So the necessary condition derived from coverage bound is not sufficient, and we need to determine exactly which k are possible.

Let's compute the bound from coverage inequality for odd n. n = 2m+1. Then M_n = (n+1)/2 = m+1. Then LHS = (n - k) * n + k * M_n = n^2 - nk + k (m+1) = n^2 + k (m+1 - n). Since n = 2m+1, m+1 - n = m+1 - (2m+1) = -m. So LHS = n^2 - k m. Also n(n+1)/2 = (2m+1)(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1? Let's compute: (2m+1)(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)(m+1). That's (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1.

Now LHS = n^2 - k m = (2m+1)^2 - k m = 4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m.

We need LHS ≥ (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1.

So:

4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m ≥ 2m^2 + 3m + 1 → 4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m - 2m^2 - 3m - 1 ≥ 0 → (2m^2 + m) - k m ≥ 0 → 2m^2 + m ≥ k m → if m > 0, divide by m: 2m + 1 ≥ k → k ≤ 2m + 1 = n.

So that bound gives k ≤ n. That's trivial. So for odd n, the bound from coverage does not restrict k (except that k ≤ n). So the necessary condition from coverage inequality is not restrictive for odd n. So we need a different approach to determine possible k for odd n. For n=3, we saw k=3 works. For n=5, we can attempt to construct configurations with many sunny lines. The maximum sunny lines possible is n, but we need to see if it's possible to cover all points with n sunny lines. So it's possible in terms of bound. However, we need to see if there exist 5 sunny lines covering all 15 points. That's like a covering problem. We can try to construct using lines of slope 1 and other slopes. For n=3, we used lines with slopes 1, -1/2, -2, which are all sunny. That's 3 lines. Now, to cover all points with n lines, we need to allocate coverage. The bound we derived using coverage capacity (n - k) * n + k * M_n ≥ total points is necessary, but not sufficient. However, we can also derive a necessary condition based on the number of lines that can cover points with a certain property. Another approach is to consider the points on the boundary or the lines with slope 0, ∞, -1. Since sunny lines cannot be parallel to those, maybe there is a combinatorial argument about covering the points with a+b = constant (anti-diagonal) lines. For example, the set of points with a+b = s (for s = 2..n+1) lie on lines parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1). Those lines are not sunny. So if we have many sunny lines, they cannot cover many of those anti-diagonal points because they are not on those lines. However, a sunny line can intersect an anti-diagonal in at most one point, perhaps, but could it contain two points from different anti-diagonals? Actually any line (unless it's parallel to x+y=0) can intersect each anti-diagonal line (x+y = constant) in at most one point. So if we have a sunny line, it can contain at most one point from each anti-diagonal (since if it contains two points with the same a+b, then they'd be on the same anti-diagonal line, which would be parallel to x+y=0, but a sunny line is not parallel to that, so it can intersect that line at most once). So indeed, for a sunny line L, for each s (2..n+1), |L ∩ P_n with a+b = s| ≤ 1. So a sunny line can cover at most one point from each anti-diagonal. The anti-diagonals have sizes (s-1) for s≥2. The total points is sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = n(n+1)/2.

Now, we have n lines total, exactly k are sunny. The remaining n-k are non-sunny, which are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. Those lines can cover many points from a single anti-diagonal (since a line parallel to x+y=0 is exactly an anti-diagonal line). So if we have many non-sunny lines, they can cover many points.

Now we need a general solution. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ...". So we need to describe the set of k in terms of n. From examples:

We constructed for n=3,4,5,6,7 using vertical non-sunny lines. Let's generalize.

That is, all points with a ∈ {1,…,n-3} and b ≥ 1 with a+b ≤ n+1. The union of these vertical lines covers some points. The remaining points are those with a ≥ n-2. However, note that we also have the 3 sunny lines, which are to be chosen to cover those remaining points. But we must also consider that some points with a ≤ n-3 might not be covered by the vertical lines because they have b such that a+b ≤ n+1. But vertical lines cover all points with that a, regardless of b, as long as b is any integer (but we only care about those in P_n). Since for a fixed a, the vertical line x = a contains all points (a,b) with b integer. So if a ≤ n-3, then all b with a+b ≤ n+1 are in P_n, and they are covered by that vertical line. So b can be 1,2,3. Similarly, a = n-1 → b ≤ 2, a = n → b ≤ 1. So the uncovered points after vertical lines are:

U = { (a,b) : a ∈ {n-2, n-1, n}, b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1 }.

That's a small set. Let's list:

For a = n-2: b = 1,2,3 (if n-2 ≥ 1, which is true for n≥3). For a = n-1: b = 1,2. For a = n: b = 1.

So size of U = 3+2+1 = 6.

Thus U is the same as the set we encountered for n=6,7 with vertical non-sunny lines (but we used V1..V_{n-3}). Indeed, after V1..V_{n-3}, the uncovered points are those with a ≥ n-2. That's 6 points. So we need to cover these 6 points with 3 sunny lines. We can try to choose the 3 sunny lines as the lines through the three pairs we used earlier, but are they all sunny? They are lines with slopes 1, -1/2, -2. Are they distinct? Yes. Are they all sunny? Slopes 1, -1/2, -2 are not 0, ∞, -1. So they are sunny. But we need to check whether those lines indeed pass through the points of U. The points U are:

(n-2,1), (n-2,2), (n-2,3), (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1).

We need to find three sunny lines whose union covers these six points. Based on earlier constructions for n=5,6,7, we had:

- L1: line through (n-2,1) and (n-1,2) → slope (2-1)/((n-1)-(n-2)) = 1/1 = 1. That's sunny. Does it also pass through any other points? (n,?) but that's not needed. So L1 covers (n-2,1) and (n-1,2). 
- L2: line through (n-2,2) and (n,1) → slope (1-2)/(n-(n-2)) = -1/2. That's sunny. 
- L3: line through (n-2,3) and (n-1,1) → slope (1-3)/((n-1)-(n-2)) = -2/1 = -2. That's sunny.

But wait, we need to check if (n-2,3) is present (yes, if n-2 ≥ 1, which is true). And (n-1,1) is present. So L3 covers those two. So the union of these three lines covers all six points. Indeed, we have:

L1 covers (n-2,1) and (n-1,2)
L2 covers (n-2,2) and (n,1)
L3 covers (n-2,3) and (n-1,1)

Thus all six points covered. So k=3 works for any n≥3.

Now, is it possible to have k=4? That would require that we have 4 sunny lines and n-4 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. We need to see if there exists a set of n lines (distinct) with exactly 4 sunny covering all P_n. We can try to prove that if k ≥ 4, then it's impossible. But we also have to consider that for n=3, k=4 is impossible because there are only 3 lines, so k cannot be 4. So the statement "k can be 0,1,3 for all n≥3" holds, but we must also verify that k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. Let's test n=3, we already did. For n=4, we did. For n=5, we did. For n=6, we did. For n=7, we did. So it seems plausible. But is there a general proof? Let's attempt to derive a contradiction if k ≥ 2, but we need to exclude k=2 and k≥4. But k=3 is allowed. So the answer is likely: k ∈ {0,1,3} for all n ≥ 3. However, we should also check if k = n is possible when n=3 (yes). But for n≥4, k=n is impossible. So the answer is not simply "all k ≤ n except 2" because k=4 is >3 and impossible. So the set is {0,1,3} for all n≥3.

But we must confirm that for n=3, k=3 works, and we also have k=0,1 works. So that's covered. For n=3, does k=3 (all sunny) possible? Yes we constructed. So the answer is {0,1,3} for n≥3.

Now, we need to produce a solution with rigorous proof. Let's outline:

1. Definition of sunny.
2. Let n ≥ 3 be given.
3. Determine all k (nonnegative integers) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny satisfying covering condition.
   - Show that k = 0, 1, 3 are possible.
   - Show that for any n ≥ 3, k = 2 is impossible.
   - Show that for any n ≥ 3, k ≥ 4 is impossible.

But note: For n=3, k=3 is possible, and k=4 is impossible because there are only 3 lines. So the impossibility of k≥4 for n≥4 is enough, but we also need to argue that for n=3, k=4 is impossible anyway (since k ≤ n). So the classification can be: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n except k = 2, and also k = 4,5,...,n are impossible? But for n=4, k=3 is possible, so the impossible values are 2 and 4, but 4 is ≥4. So the statement "k ≥ 4 is impossible for all n≥3" is false because for n=3, k=3 is not ≥4, but for n=4, k=4 is impossible. So we need to state: For n≥4, k≥4 is impossible; for n=3, k=4 is impossible because k ≤ n. So overall, the possible k are 0,1,3. That's a clean answer.

But we should also consider n=3, k=3 is allowed. So answer: k = 0, 1, 3.

Now, prove impossibility of k=2 for all n≥3. We'll need a rigorous argument. Let's attempt to prove that any covering of P_n with n lines must have at least n-2 non-sunny lines (or something). But we need to show that k=2 is impossible. We can try to prove that if exactly 2 lines are sunny, then the union of all lines cannot cover all points. We can attempt a combinatorial argument.

We have n lines. Among them, 2 are sunny, the other n-2 are non-sunny. The non-sunny lines are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. They can be of three types. We need to cover all points of P_n. Let's denote the set of points P_n.

Observation: For any line L that is not parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., not slope -1), it can intersect each anti-diagonal (line x+y = constant) in at most one point. For a sunny line, it's not slope -1, so it can contain at most one point from each anti-diagonal. Also, a line that is parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1) can contain many points (all with same a+b). But such lines are not sunny.

Now, the anti-diagonal with largest sum is s = n+1, which contains n points. That's a line (non-sunny). To cover those n points, we could either include that anti-diagonal as a non-sunny line, or cover them with other lines. Since we have only n lines, if we don't include that anti-diagonal, we would need to cover those n points using the other n-1 lines. Each of those lines can cover at most one point from that anti-diagonal (because they are not parallel to it, and if they are parallel, they would be slope -1, but then they'd be non-sunny; but we could have multiple slope -1 lines, each covering a different anti-diagonal, but they cannot cover points from this anti-diagonal if they are different constants. Actually a slope -1 line with constant c (x+y=c) intersects the anti-diagonal x+y=n+1 only if c = n+1. So only that line itself covers those points. Any other line (including sunny) intersects it in at most one point. So if we have 2 sunny lines, they can cover at most 2 points from that anti-diagonal. The non-sunny lines, if they are not that anti-diagonal, could also cover at most 1 point each. But we have n-2 non-sunny lines. So total points covered from that anti-diagonal is at most 2 (from sunny) + (n-2)*1 (from non-sunny) = n, but we need to cover all n points. So if all non-sunny lines are not that anti-diagonal, then the maximum coverage is 2 + (n-2) = n, which is enough if they are disjoint. However, we must also consider that the anti-diagonal itself might be included as a non-sunny line. If it is included, then we have n-1 other lines (including sunny). That would cover all n points trivially. So the impossibility of k=2 must arise from some other property, not just covering the largest anti-diagonal. Because with n-2 non-sunny lines, we can have some of them be that anti-diagonal, and then the rest can be other lines. So we need a more refined argument.

Let's attempt to prove that if k=2, then there are at most n-2 non-sunny lines, but they can be chosen to include the anti-diagonal. So maybe the impossibility is due to covering other points. For n=3, we saw that with 2 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny, it's impossible. For n=4, with 2 sunny and 2 non-sunny, it's impossible. So there is some structural obstruction.

Perhaps we can consider the points with a+b = n+1 (largest sum). That's n points. They lie on a line L0 (slope -1). If we include L0 as a non-sunny line, then we have n-1 other lines. Among them, 2 are sunny, the rest are non-sunny. That seems plausible. So why would k=2 be impossible? So the best is to use non-sunny lines that are axes (horizontals/verticals) to cover many points. That's what we considered. So the bound on coverage of non-sunny lines is limited. Another approach: Use the concept of "sunny lines" are those that are not parallel to the axes or the line x+y=0. I recall a known problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be an integer. Determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny covering all lattice points of the triangle with vertices (1,1), (n,1), (1,n) or something." Something like that. The solution might involve considering the points with a+b = constant and using a pigeonhole principle on the number of sunny lines needed to cover them. Let's think deeper.

We have P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a,b ≥ 1, a+b ≤ n+1}. We need to cover P_n with n lines. Let L_1,…,L_n be distinct lines. Among them, k are sunny. The remaining n−k are non-sunny. We need to determine for which k such a covering exists.

We already have constructions for k=0,1,3. We suspect that k=2 is impossible. Also k≥4 is impossible for n≥4, but we need to prove that for any n≥3, if k≥4, then it's impossible (except maybe n=3 where k=3 is allowed). But we have a construction with k=3. So the answer is {0,1,3}. To prove impossibility of k≥4, we can argue that the total number of points is n(n+1)/2, and if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * M_n points (if they are disjoint). But we also have non-sunny lines. However, we need to show that if k≥4, then even with the best possible arrangement (i.e., maximizing coverage), the union cannot be all points. But perhaps we can prove a stronger bound: The sum of the maximum possible coverage of lines (with sunny lines having M_n, non-sunny having n) is something like (n - k) * n + k * M_n. For n≥3, if k≥4, then this bound is < |P_n|. That would be a simple necessary condition. Let's test.

Compute |P_n| = n(n+1)/2.

Compute bound B(k) = (n - k) * n + k * M_n, where M_n = floor((n+1)/2). For n even, n=2m: M_n = m, B(k) = (2m - k)*2m + k*m = 4m^2 - 2mk + km = 4m^2 - km. For n odd, n=2m+1: M_n = m+1, B(k) = (2m+1 - k)*(2m+1) + k*(m+1) = (2m+1)^2 - k(2m+1) + k(m+1) = (2m+1)^2 - k(2m+1 - m -1) = (2m+1)^2 - k(m). So B(k) = (2m+1)^2 - k*m.

We want to see for which k does B(k) < |P_n|. If B(k) < |P_n|, then it's impossible because the union cannot exceed B(k). However, B(k) is an upper bound; if B(k) < |P_n|, then indeed impossible. If B(k) ≥ |P_n|, it doesn't guarantee existence. So we can use this necessary condition to rule out k≥4 for all n≥3? Let's compute.

For n even, n=2m, |P_n| = (2m)(2m+1)/2 = m(2m+1). B(k) = 4m^2 - k*m. We need to compare 4m^2 - k*m with m(2m+1). 4m^2 - k*m ≥ m(2m+1) ⇒ 4m^2 - k*m ≥ 2m^2 + m ⇒ 2m^2 - k*m - m ≥ 0 ⇒ 2m^2 - m(k+1) ≥ 0 ⇒ m(2m - (k+1)) ≥ 0 ⇒ 2m ≥ k+1 ⇒ k ≤ 2m - 1 = n - 1.

So B(k) ≥ |P_n| when k ≤ n-1. For k = n-1, B(n-1) = 4m^2 - (2m-1)*m = 4m^2 - 2m^2 + m = 2m^2 + m = m(2m+1) = |P_n|. So equality. For k = n (which is 2m), then B(n) = 4m^2 - 2m*m = 4m^2 - 2m^2 = 2m^2, which is less than m(2m+1) for m≥2? For m=2 (n=4), 2m^2 = 8, |P_n| = 10, so B(n) < |P_n|. But we only have n lines, so k cannot be n because there are only n lines, but k is number of sunny lines, so k ≤ n. For n=4, k=4 is impossible because B(4)=8<10. For n=6, k=6 would be B(6)=2*9=18 < 21. So that's a necessary condition. For n odd, n=2m+1, |P_n| = (2m+1)(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)(m+1) = (2m+1)(m+1). B(k) = (2m+1)^2 - k*m. We want to see when B(k) < |P_n|. Compute |P_n| - B(k) = (2m+1)(m+1) - ((2m+1)^2 - k*m) = (2m+1)(m+1) - (2m+1)^2 + k*m = (2m+1)[(m+1) - (2m+1)] + k*m = (2m+1)(-m) + k*m = -m(2m+1) + k*m = m(k - (2m+1)). So B(k) < |P_n| iff k < 2m+1 = n. Since k ≤ n, B(k) ≥ |P_n| for k = n? For k = n, B(n) = (2m+1)^2 - n*m = (2m+1)^2 - (2m+1)*m = (2m+1)(2m+1 - m) = (2m+1)(m+1) = |P_n|. So equality. For k = n-1 (which is 2m), B(2m) = (2m+1)^2 - 2m*m = (4m^2+4m+1) - 2m^2 = 2m^2+4m+1. |P_n| = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2+3m+1. Compare: B(2m) = 2m^2+4m+1 > 2m^2+3m+1 for m≥1. So B(k) ≥ |P_n| for all k ≤ n? Actually we need to check the sign: For k = n-1 (2m), B(2m) > |P_n|. So the necessary condition B(k) ≥ |P_n| is satisfied for all k ≤ n, but we want to find k for which B(k) < |P_n|, which would be impossible. For n odd, we have B(n) = |P_n|, so k = n is possible from capacity perspective. But we also have a construction with k = n (all sunny) for n=3 works, but for n=5, we saw k=n=5 is impossible because we couldn't cover all points. The capacity bound B(5) for n=5 (m=2) is |P_n| = 15, so capacity doesn't rule out. But we need to prove impossibility for n≥5 when k≥4? Actually for n=5, k=4 is impossible, but k=5 is also impossible? Let's test with capacity bound for n=5, k=5: B(5) = (2m+1)^2 - k*m = 25 - 5*2 = 25 - 10 = 15 = |P_n|. So capacity doesn't rule out. So the impossibility of k=5 for n=5 is not captured by this bound; we need a different argument.

Thus the bound B(k) is only necessary; it doesn't give contradiction for k≥4 when k=n. For odd n≥5, we need to prove that k=n is impossible. For n=3, it's possible. So the answer is not simply all k except 2; we need to determine all k that are possible. The pattern we observed: for n=3, possible k = 0,1,3; for n=4, possible k = 0,1,3; for n=5, possible k = 0,1,3; for n=6, possible k = 0,1,3; for n=7, possible k = 0,1,3. We need to cover these with 3 non-sunny lines. Is there a set of 3 lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal) that covers all these 12 points? Let's try to see if we can take some horizontals and verticals. H1 (b=1) covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). That's 5 points, includes (2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). H2 (b=2) covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2). But (2,2) and (3,2) are excluded, so H2 would cover (1,2),(4,2) and also (2,2),(3,2) which are excluded. But we are free to choose lines; if a line covers an excluded point, then that point would be covered by a non-sunny line, which would be okay because it's covered, but we need to exclude those points from the union? Actually we are constructing a covering. The covering condition is that each point lies on at least one line. If we want to have a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines, we can choose any lines. The complement T (points not covered by non-sunny lines) must be covered by sunny lines. So if we include a non-sunny line that covers some of the points we want to be in T, that's okay as long as those points are covered. But we are trying to see if a configuration with k=2 exists. So we are free to choose non-sunny lines and sunny lines. The set T is defined as P_n \ (union of non-sunny lines). If we have a covering, then T must be a subset of union of sunny lines. So if we try to design a covering with k=2, we can try to pick non-sunny lines that cover many points, leaving a small T, which we then cover with 2 sunny lines. The earlier bound on union of n-2 non-sunny lines suggests that the maximum size of union is (n(n+1)/2) - 3. So the minimum possible size of T is 3. So to achieve a covering with k=2, we must have T of size exactly 3 (or larger, but then covering it with 2 sunny lines seems impossible). So we need to examine whether there exists a choice of n-2 non-sunny lines such that T is of size 3 and also those 3 points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. If such exists, then k=2 is possible. If not, then k=2 impossible. But we also have to consider that T could be larger than 3, but then covering it with 2 sunny lines would be even more difficult. So it suffices to prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement T has size at least 3, and additionally, any 2 sunny lines cannot cover T. But we cannot claim that any 2 sunny lines cannot cover T if T is larger than 3; we need to prove that it's impossible. Perhaps we can prove a stronger bound: the total number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is at most something, and that this something is less than the minimum possible size of T? That seems messy.

Alternate approach: Use the concept of "sunny lines" are those that are not parallel to axes or x+y=0. So any line that is not parallel to those three directions is called sunny. Consider the set of lines L_i. Among them, 2 are sunny. The rest are non-sunny. Now, note that any point (a,b) in P_n with a+b = s (where s from 2 to n+1) lies on exactly one line with slope -1 (the anti-diagonal). If we have a sunny line L, then it can intersect that anti-diagonal at most once. So L can contain at most 1 point from each anti-diagonal. That's trivial.

Now, perhaps we can prove that if there are only 2 sunny lines, then the number of points that can be covered by the non-sunny lines is at most something, and then the 2 sunny lines can cover at most something, and total < |P_n|. But we need to bound the union of all lines (including sunny) by something. We can bound the union by (n-2)*n + 2*M_n. That's an upper bound. For n≥3, if k=2, then (n-2)*n + 2*M_n could be ≥ |P_n|? Let's compute. For n even, n=2m, M_n = m, B(2) = (2m-2)*2m + 2*m = 4m^2 - 4m + 2m = 4m^2 - 2m. |P_n| = m(2m+1) = 2m^2 + m. Compare: 4m^2 - 2m vs 2m^2 + m → 4m^2 - 2m - (2m^2 + m) = 2m^2 - 3m = m(2m - 3). For m≥2 (n≥4), this is positive, so B(2) > |P_n|. For n=4 (m=2), B(2) = 4*4 - 4 = 16 - 4 = 12? Actually compute: (2m-2)*2m + 2*m = (4-2)*4 + 2*2 = 2*4 + 4 = 8+4=12. |P_n|=10. So bound is higher. So capacity not limiting. For n odd, n=2m+1, B(2) = (2m+1 - 2)*(2m+1) + 2*(m+1) = (2m-1)*(2m+1) + 2m+2 = (4m^2 - 1) + 2m+2 = 4m^2 + 2m + 1. |P_n| = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2+3m+1. Compare: B(2) - |P_n| = (4m^2+2m+1) - (2m^2+3m+1) = 2m^2 - m = m(2m-1). For m≥1 (n≥3), this is ≥1. So bound > |P_n|. So not contradictory.

Thus the necessary condition doesn't exclude k=2. So we need a more sophisticated argument.

Let's try to prove impossibility of k=2 by considering the points on the boundary of the triangle, like the points with a=1 or b=1. There are 2n-1 such points? The non-sunny lines we can use are, for example, horizontals or verticals covering some interior points. But we need to see if it's possible. This is getting complicated.

I recall a known problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be an integer. Determine all k for which there exist n distinct lines such that every lattice point of the triangle with vertices (1,1), (n,1), (1,n) lies on at least one line, and exactly k of the lines are sunny." The solution probably involves considering the lines that are not sunny (parallel to axes or x+y=0). There are n lines total, so the number of non-sunny lines is n−k. If we have many sunny lines, then the number of non-sunny lines is small. But we can use non-sunny lines to cover many points. For k=3, we used 3 sunny lines and n-3 non-sunny lines. That's a construction. For k=2, we would have n-2 non-sunny lines. The maximum coverage by n-2 non-sunny lines is (n(n+1)/2) - 3. That's still less than total points, leaving at least 3 points uncovered. Those points must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. So we need to see if it's possible that the 2 sunny lines cover those 3 points. The three points are those not lying on any of the chosen non-sunny lines. We can try to argue that those three points are not collinear and also cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines because any line passing through two of them would be non-sunny. Let's prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement T (points not covered) must contain at least 3 points that are pairwise not on a sunny line, i.e., no sunny line contains two of them. That seems plausible if the complement is exactly the set of points with large coordinates. But is it always that the complement T is a subset of the "corner" region where points have coordinates (a,b) with a+b large and a,b small? Actually the complement after taking n-2 horizontals is the set of points with b≥n-1. That's a small set. Could we choose non-sunny lines such that the complement T is larger than 3 but still coverable by 2 sunny lines? That seems unlikely because if T is larger, it's even harder. So the most promising approach is to prove that in any configuration with k=2, the union of the non-sunny lines cannot cover all points, and additionally, the complement T cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. But we need a proof that T must be a set of points that is "sunny-free" in the sense that no sunny line contains two points of T. That seems to be a property of T derived from the structure of non-sunny lines. Let's try to analyze.

For example, could we take some horizontals and some anti-diagonals such that the union is maximal and the complement is something like {(1,2),(2,3),(3,4)}? Let's test for n=5, m=3. Thus it seems that any maximal union of n-2 non-sunny lines yields a complement that is a set of 3 points that are pairwise not on a sunny line. That is a strong property. If we can prove that for any set of n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement T (points not covered) must have at least 3 points, and additionally, any 2 sunny lines cannot cover T because any line containing two points of T would be non-sunny, but we need to show that T cannot contain a pair that is collinear on a sunny line. That seems plausible because the points of T are "far" from the axes and have coordinates such that they lie in the "corner" region where they are either all on the same anti-diagonal or on a boundary. But is it always true that T contains a pair that is not on a sunny line? Let's try to see if there is a scenario where T has size 3 and contains a sunny pair. For that to happen, the three points must be such that two are collinear on a sunny line. That means they are not on an axis or anti-diagonal. But can such a pair exist while still being uncovered by non-sunny lines? The non-sunny lines are those that are parallel to axes or anti-diagonal. If two points are on a sunny line, then they are not on the same axis or anti-diagonal, so they are not covered by any line that is an axis or anti-diagonal. So they could be uncovered. So it's possible that T contains a sunny pair. But we need to see if such a T can be the complement of n-2 non-sunny lines. The complement T is defined as P_n \ (union of non-sunny lines). So if T contains a sunny pair, then those points are not covered by any non-sunny line. That's fine. But we also need that all other points are covered. So the question reduces to: can we choose n-2 non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal) such that the set of uncovered points is exactly a specific triple (with a sunny pair) and the lines are distinct? If such a covering exists, then k=2 would be possible. So we need to either construct such a covering for all n≥3, or prove that it's impossible.

Given the pattern we observed, it seems that the maximal unions (size (n(n+1)/2)-3) always produce complements that are "corner" triples with no sunny pair. But could there be a maximal union that yields a different triple, perhaps with a sunny pair? Let's examine the structure. We have m = n-2 non-sunny lines. To maximize coverage, we want lines that cover as many points as possible. The points are in a triangle. The axes lines are dense. The anti-diagonals are also dense but they are not parallel to axes, they are slope -1. However, the maximum coverage is achieved by taking lines that are "staircase" covering. I'd like to prove a general lemma: For any set of m non-sunny lines, the union size ≤ (n(n+1)/2) - 3, with equality only if the lines are all either horizontal covering b=1..m or vertical covering a=1..m, or some combination that still achieves equality but yields a complement that is a triple with no sunny pair. But is it possible to achieve equality with a different set that yields a triple with a sunny pair? Let's try to search for n=6, m=4. That seems unlikely because those 3 points are far from the axes? Actually (1,2) lies on a=1, which is a vertical line. If we include a=1 as a non-sunny line, then (1,2) is covered. So to avoid covering T, we cannot have any non-sunny line that goes through any of those points. That means we cannot have any line with slope 0, ∞, or -1 that passes through any of those points. So we need to cover all other points with lines that avoid those three points. Is that possible? Let's try to see if we can cover P_6 \ T with 4 non-sunny lines. The points of P_6 are many. T is a small set. The complement (points to cover) is 18 points. We need to find 4 lines (horizontal, vertical, anti-diagonal) that cover all those 18 points and avoid T. That seems challenging. But we don't need to prove impossibility for all n; we can prove a necessary condition based on the parity or counting of points on each anti-diagonal, and then show that if k=2, then the 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points because they would have to cover at least 3 points that are all on a line with slope -1 (the largest anti-diagonal) or something.

Let's try a different approach. Consider the points on the anti-diagonal with sum s = n+1. There are n points. Suppose we have k sunny lines. Each sunny line can contain at most 1 point from this anti-diagonal. Therefore, the number of sunny lines that can cover points from this anti-diagonal is at most n. That's trivial. But we need to cover all n points on that anti-diagonal. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then they cover at most 2 points from that anti-diagonal. The remaining n-2 points must be covered by the non-sunny lines. Since non-sunny lines are parallel to axes or slope -1. The anti-diagonal itself is a line with slope -1, so it's non-sunny. If we include that anti-diagonal as one of the non-sunny lines, then it covers all n points. That's one line. Then we have n-3 other non-sunny lines. So the problem is that with k=2, we have n-2 non-sunny lines. That seems not.

Maybe we can prove that the number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is at most something, and that this something is less than the total points minus the coverage of non-sunny lines, but we need a contradiction. Another idea: Use the fact that each non-sunny line is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Consider the set of points with a+b = s for s = 2,...,n+1. We can try a double counting argument using the concept of "sunny lines" as lines that are not parallel to axes or x+y=0. Consider the set of lines L_i. Among them, 2 are sunny. The other n-2 are non-sunny. Now, note that any point (a,b) with a,b ≥ 1 lies on exactly one line with slope -1 (x+y = constant). The bound on union of non-sunny lines suggests that the maximum possible size of union is (n(n+1)/2) - 3. So if the union is maximal, then T has size 3. Let's step back and think about the problem more systematically. The problem is likely from an olympiad. I recall a problem: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to the x-axis, y-axis, or the line x+y=0. Let n ≥ 3 be an integer. Determine all k for which there exist n distinct lines such that every lattice point of the triangle with vertices (1,1), (n,1), (1,n) lies on at least one line, and exactly k of the lines are sunny." We need to solve a combinatorial geometry / covering problem. Let me restate:

We have n >= 3 given integer. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying:
- For all positive integers a and b with a+b <= n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines.

Definition: A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (since x+y=0 => y = -x, slope -1). Actually "parallel to the line x+y=0" means lines of slope -1, i.e., lines of the form y = -x + c. So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal (slope 0), not vertical (infinite slope), and not with slope -1. So they can be any other slope, and also can be any line (including those that are not parallel to those three). However note that they must be distinct lines.

We need to determine all possible k (nonnegative integers) for which there exists a set of n distinct lines (in the plane) with exactly k sunny lines, and such that every point (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b <= n+1 lies on at least one of those lines.

6 (2,2) not covered
7 (2,3) not covered
8 (3,1) covered
9 (3,2) not covered
10 (4,1) covered

So uncovered: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. With 2 sunny lines, max coverage is 4 points, so it's possible in principle. We need to see if there exist two sunny lines covering these 3 points. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, we need a matching covering them. But we also need to consider that the sunny lines must be distinct from the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are y=1 (horizontal) and x=1 (vertical). Sunny lines can have slopes not 0, ∞, -1. So can we find two sunny lines covering {(2,2),(2,3),(3,2)}? Let's examine possible pairs:

- (2,2) and (2,3) are vertical, not sunny.
- (2,2) and (3,2) are horizontal, not sunny.
- (2,3) and (3,2) have slope -1, not sunny.

So any line containing two of these points is non-sunny. Therefore, any sunny line can contain at most one of these points. So with two sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 points. Hence at least one point remains uncovered. So that fails.

What if we choose a different pair of non-sunny lines, like a slope -1 line and a horizontal line, etc. We need to cover all points. Perhaps with 2 non-sunny lines, we can cover many points, leaving only 2 points uncovered, which can be covered by 2 sunny lines. Let's explore systematically.

We have 10 points. The non-sunny lines can be any of the three families. The maximum coverage per line is 4. So with 2 non-sunny lines, we can cover at most 8 points if they are disjoint, but they might overlap, reducing distinct coverage. The remaining points are 10 - (distinct covered). We need to cover those with 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points, we need the remaining points to be at most 4. But also they must be coverable by two sunny lines without violating the sunny condition.

We can try to design a configuration where the non-sunny lines are, say, the line x+y=4 (covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)) and the line x+y=5 (covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)). But x+y=4 and x+y=5 are both slope -1 (non-sunny). That's two non-sunny lines. Let's see which points they cover:

- x+y=4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)
- x+y=5: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)

Union: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. Missing (1,1),(1,2),(2,1). That's 3 points. With 2 sunny lines, can we cover those 3 points? As before, any line containing two of those points is non-sunny. So at most 2 points can be covered. So fails.

What about using a horizontal line y=1 and a slope -1 line x+y=5 (covers 4 points). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Union covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2). That's 7 points. Missing (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). That's 3 points. Again, those 3 points are (1,2),(1,3),(2,2). Are there any sunny lines covering two of them? (1,2)-(1,3) vertical, non-sunny; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal; (1,3)-(2,2) slope -1. So no. So with 2 sunny lines, can't cover 3 points. So fails.

What about using a vertical line x=1 and a slope -1 line x+y=5. x=1 covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). x+y=5 covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Union: (1,1)-(1,4) and (2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 + 3 = 7 points (overlap at (1,4)). Missing (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 3 points. Again, those 3 points are (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Pairs: (2,1)-(2,2) vertical; (2,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1. So all non-sunny. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points covered. So fails.

What about using a horizontal line y=2 and a vertical line x=2? That's two non-sunny lines. y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2) (3 points). x=2 covers (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) (3 points). Union: (1,2),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2) → 5 points. Missing (1,1),(1,3),(1,4),(3,1),(4,1). That's 5 points. Too many. Sunny lines would need to cover 5 points with 2 lines, impossible.

Thus it seems k=2 might be impossible for n=4. But we need a rigorous proof for general n. The problem asks to determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny satisfying covering condition. So we need to find, for a given n≥3, which k are possible. We've seen that k=0 is possible. For n=3, k=1 and k=3 are possible, but k=2 is not. For n=4, k=0,3 (and maybe 1,2?) We constructed k=3 works. Let's test k=2 for n=4 more systematically with other choices of non-sunny lines. Could we have a configuration with 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines such that the union covers all 10 points? The two non-sunny lines could be chosen to cover many points. The maximum distinct coverage by two non-sunny lines is 8 (if they are disjoint and each cover 4 points). But can we get 8? For example, take horizontal line y=1 covering 4 points, and horizontal line y=2 covering 3 points, but they are both horizontal (non-sunny). That's 2 non-sunny lines, covering 4+3=7 points (overlap at (1,2)? Actually y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). No overlap. So distinct coverage 7. The remaining points are (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) maybe? Let's list all points:

(1,1) y=1
(1,2) y=2
(1,3) not covered
(1,4) not covered
(2,1) y=1
(2,2) y=2
(2,3) not covered
(3,1) y=1
(3,2) y=2
(4,1) y=1

So uncovered: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2). That's 4 points. With 2 sunny lines, each covering at most 2 points, we could cover all 4 if we can pair them appropriately with sunny lines. Are there sunny lines covering pairs among these 4? The points: A=(1,3), B=(1,4), C=(2,3), D=(3,2). Check pairs:

- A(1,3) and C(2,3): horizontal (y=3) → not sunny.
- A and D(3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-1) = -1/2 → sunny.
- B(1,4) and C(2,3): slope (3-4)/(2-1) = -1 → not sunny.
- B and D(3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- C(2,3) and D(3,2): slope -1 → not sunny.
- A and B: vertical → not sunny.
- C and D: slope -1.
- A and C: horizontal.
- B and D: slope -1.
- A and D: sunny.
- B and C: not sunny.

So only pair with sunny line is (A,D) (1,3)-(3,2) with slope -1/2. That's one sunny line covering 2 points. The other pair (B,C) is not sunny, so cannot be used. So with 2 sunny lines, we can cover at most 2 of those points (if we pair (A,D) together). Then we need to cover B and C as well. They could be covered by a sunny line that passes through them, but that line is not sunny, so cannot be a sunny line. Could we cover B with one sunny line and C with another sunny line, but that would require two sunny lines, but we have only 2 sunny lines total. If we use one sunny line for (A,D), then we have one sunny line left. It can cover at most one of B or C, leaving the other uncovered. So fails. Could we instead use a sunny line that covers (B,C) but that's not sunny. So no.

Thus k=2 fails for n=4. So possible k are 0 and n-1? But we also have n=3, n-1=2, but we found k=2 impossible. For n=3, n-1=2 fails, but k=3 works. So the pattern is not simply n-1. For n=3, k=3 works. For n=4, k=3 works. For n=5, maybe k=4 works? But we need to determine all k for general n. Let's try to compute maximum coverage per sunny line for n. For a line L that is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1, the maximum number of integer points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤n+1 that lie on L is something like floor((n+1)/2) for slope 1? Actually we need to find the maximum possible size of intersection of such a line with P. This is a combinatorial geometry problem. Let's denote P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. We need to find, for a line L (any real line), the maximum number of points from P_n that lie on L. Then we can bound the covering capability.

For n≥3, we can try to determine the maximum possible number of points from P_n on a line that is not parallel to axes or x+y=0. Since we are looking for possible k, we can consider constructing configurations with many sunny lines. The constraints might be that the number of sunny lines k must be at most n-2? Or at most something like n - ceil((n+1)/2)? But we need to analyze.

Observation: The set P_n is a right triangle of lattice points. Lines that are not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 can have at most something like n points? Actually we saw for n=4, max is 2. For n=3, max is 2. For n=5, let's compute. P_5: a,b≥1, a+b≤6. Points: a=1..5, b=1..5 but with a+b≤6. That's triangular number 15. Let's compute max points on a sunny line. The maximum per line among all lines (including non-sunny) is n (if we take a horizontal line y=c, with c from 1 to n, it covers (n+1-c) points, which is ≤ n, but when c=1, covers n points; when c=n, covers 1 point). So the maximum is n. For sunny lines, they cannot be horizontal/vertical/slope -1. But maybe they can still cover n points if they are, say, lines with slope 0? That's horizontal, not sunny. Slope ∞ vertical, not sunny. Slope -1 not sunny. So those are excluded. So we need to find the maximum coverage among lines with slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

Consider a line with slope m. The integer points on it are those with x integer and y integer, which occur if m is rational and intercept appropriate, else at most one point. So to maximize coverage, we want m rational with small denominator, and we want the line to intersect P_n in as many lattice points as possible. The worst case (for covering) is when the line is such that many points lie on it. The line y = x + c has slope 1. The number of points from P_n on it is the number of a ≥ 1 such that a + (a + c) ≤ n+1 → 2a ≤ n+1 - c → a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/2). So max when c is as small as possible, but c must be integer if we want integer points? Actually we can choose c to be any real number, but to get integer points, we need that the line passes through integer points. If c is not integer, then for integer a, y = a + c is not integer (unless c is integer). So to have many integer points, we need c to be integer. For slope 1, the line y = x + c with c integer will contain integer points (a, a+c) for all integer a. Then the number of such points within P_n is as derived, max when c is small (like 0) gives a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So max count = ceil((n+1)/2)? Actually if n is odd, (n+1)/2 is integer. For n=3, (n+1)/2 = 2 → 2 points. For n=4, (n+1)/2 = 2.5 → floor = 2 → 2 points. So max is floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2.

Now consider a line with slope 2 (y = 2x + c). For integer c, points are (a, 2a + c) for integer a. Condition a + (2a + c) = 3a + c ≤ n+1 → a ≤ floor((n+1 - c)/3). Max when c is small, e.g., c = 0 → a ≤ floor((n+1)/3). That's about n/3. So smaller.

Thus the maximum possible number of points from P_n on a line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 appears to be achieved by slope 1 (or slope -1 but -1 is not sunny). So the maximum is floor((n+1)/2). But we also need to consider lines with slope like 1/2? That's also rational, denominator 2. For slope 1/2, points occur when x is even. The line y = (1/2)x + c. For integer c, points (2t, t + c) maybe. Let's analyze: For a line with rational slope p/q in lowest terms, integer points exist when x is a multiple of q. Then the number of integer points within a finite interval is roughly (range of x)/q. For slope 1/2, q=2, so about n/2 points. That could be similar to slope 1. But also we need to consider that the line might not be able to contain many points if the intercept is chosen appropriately to have integer points. For slope 1/2, if we take c = 0, then points (2,1),(4,2),(6,3)... So the count is floor(n/2). For n=4, floor(4/2)=2. That's also 2. For n=3, floor(3/2)=1. So slope 1/2 gives fewer. So slope 1 gives larger coverage.

Now consider slope -1/2 (which is sunny). That's similar to slope 1/2 but negative. It will also have about n/2 points if the intercept is chosen appropriately. But for n=4, we saw that line through (1,3)-(3,2) (slope -1/2) covers two points. That's n/2? For n=4, n/2=2. So indeed that's also 2. So the maximum coverage among sunny lines might be floor((n+1)/2) or maybe ceil((n+1)/2)? Let's compute for n=4, floor((n+1)/2) = floor(2.5)=2. That's achieved by slope 1 (y=x) with c=0 gives 2 points. So that's the maximum. For n=3, floor((n+1)/2)=2. Achieved by slope 1 with c=0. For n=5, floor((n+1)/2)=3. Slope 1 with c=0 gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) → 3 points. That's within P_5 (a+b ≤6). (3,3) sum=6, ok. So max is 3. Could a sunny line with slope 1/2 cover 3 points? For n=5, slope 1/2 gives points (2,1),(4,2),(6,3) but (6,3) a+b=9>6, so only (2,1) and (4,2) are in P_5, that's 2 points. So less.

Thus we have a bound: For a sunny line L, |P_n ∩ L| ≤ floor((n+1)/2). Let's denote M_n = floor((n+1)/2). For n=3, M=2; n=4, M=2; n=5, M=3; etc.

Now, we have n lines total. Let S be the set of sunny lines (size k). Then the union of all lines covers P_n. The sunny lines contribute at most k * M_n points (counting multiplicities), but distinct points are at most that. However, some points might be covered by non-sunny lines as well, so the total number of distinct points covered by all lines is at most (n - k) * (max coverage per non-sunny line) + k * M_n, but non-sunny lines can cover up to n points (if horizontal/vertical/slope -1). However, we need to cover all |P_n| = n(n+1)/2 points. So we need to see if there is a feasible configuration for given k. It might be that for large k, it's impossible because the sunny lines cannot cover enough points, but the non-sunny lines are limited in number (n - k). So we can derive an inequality.

Let L_non be the set of non-sunny lines (size n - k). Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (achieved by e.g., y=1, which covers n points). Actually does a line parallel to x-axis (horizontal) cover at most n points? For a horizontal line y = c, with c integer from 1 to n, the points with b = c and a ≥ 1, a ≤ n+1 - c. So number of points = n+1 - c, which is ≤ n. For c=1, it's n. So max = n. Similarly vertical lines x = c cover n+1 - c points, max n. Slope -1 lines x+y = c cover c-1 points, max n (when c = n+1). So indeed, each non-sunny line can cover at most n points. So the total distinct points covered by all non-sunny lines is at most (n - k) * n. However, they may overlap, so the union size ≤ (n - k) * n.

Now the sunny lines cover at most k * M_n points (distinct). So the union of all lines is at most (n - k) * n + k * M_n. We need this to be at least |P_n| = n(n+1)/2. Thus a necessary condition is:

(n - k) * n + k * M_n ≥ n(n+1)/2.

We can compute M_n = floor((n+1)/2). Let's denote:

If n is even, say n = 2m. Then n+1 = 2m+1, floor((n+1)/2) = floor((2m+1)/2) = m. So M_n = m = n/2. If n is odd, n = 2m+1. Then n+1 = 2m+2, floor((n+1)/2) = m+1 = (n+1)/2. So M_n = (n+1)/2.

Now compute LHS: (n - k) * n + k * M_n = n^2 - nk + k M_n = n^2 + k (M_n - n).

Since M_n ≤ n/2 for even n, M_n - n is negative. For odd n, M_n = (n+1)/2, so M_n - n = (n+1)/2 - n = (n+1 - 2n)/2 = (1 - n)/2, which is negative. So LHS is decreasing in k, because as k increases, we replace non-sunny lines (which can cover up to n points) with sunny lines (which can cover at most M_n points). Since we have a fixed total number of lines n, the maximum coverage per line among non-sunny is n, among sunny is M_n. So to maximize coverage, we want as many non-sunny lines as possible. So if k is large, LHS becomes smaller. So the necessary condition is an upper bound on k.

Let's compute:

Case n even: n = 2m. Then M_n = m = n/2. So LHS = (n - k) * n + k * (n/2) = n^2 - nk + (nk/2) = n^2 - (nk/2). So LHS = n^2 - (n k)/2.

We need LHS ≥ n(n+1)/2 = (n^2 + n)/2. So:

n^2 - (n k)/2 ≥ (n^2 + n)/2.

Multiply by 2: 2n^2 - nk ≥ n^2 + n → 2n^2 - nk - n^2 - n ≥ 0 → n^2 - nk - n ≥ 0 → n (n - k - 1) ≥ 0 → n - k - 1 ≥ 0 → k ≤ n - 1.

So necessary condition: k ≤ n - 1.

But we also have k ≤ n obviously. So this says k ≤ n - 1. That is, if n is even, k cannot be n. That's consistent with our earlier observation that k=n is impossible for n≥4 (n even). For n=3 (odd), we found k=n=3 is possible. So the even case gives a bound that k ≤ n-1. That's a necessary condition, but we need to determine whether all k ≤ n-1 are possible? For n even, we have k=0 possible, k=1 possible? Let's test n=4, we constructed k=3 (which is n-1) and k=0. What about k=1? Could we construct a configuration with 1 sunny line and 3 non-sunny lines covering all points? We need to see if that's possible. For n=4, k=1 would be 1 sunny line, 3 non-sunny lines. The necessary condition from coverage inequality is k ≤ n-1 = 3, so k=1 is not ruled out. But we need to check existence. We can try to construct. We have 4 lines total. Suppose we take two non-sunny lines that cover many points, but we have 3 non-sunny lines, so we have many non-sunny lines. That seems easy: e.g., use all horizontal lines y=1,2,3,4 (4 lines) would be k=0, but we need exactly 1 sunny. Could we replace one horizontal line with a sunny line and still cover all points? For n=4, we already have a configuration with k=3 (3 sunny). That's more sunny. For k=1, we need to cover all points with 3 non-sunny lines and 1 sunny line. The non-sunny lines can be, say, three horizontal lines y=1,2,3 (cover points with b≤3). That would cover many points, but maybe not all points with b=4 (a=1). That point (1,4) would be uncovered. Could we cover it with the sunny line? The sunny line must be such that it passes through (1,4) and also perhaps other points. That's possible. But we also need to cover points with b=4 and a>1? There are none because a+b≤5, so a≤1 for b=4. So only (1,4). So we can have sunny line passing through (1,4). That's one line. Then we have three non-sunny lines covering the rest. But are three non-sunny lines enough to cover all points with b≤3? The points with b=1,2,3. The number of points is 4+3+2+1? Actually total points is 10. If we take three horizontal lines y=1,2,3, they cover points: y=1: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1); y=2: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2); y=3: (1,3),(2,3). That's 4+3+2=9 points. The missing point is (1,4). That's covered by the sunny line. So that configuration works: L1: y=1 (non-sunny), L2: y=2 (non-sunny), L3: y=3 (non-sunny), L4: some sunny line covering (1,4). But is L4 sunny? It's a line passing through (1,4) and maybe some other points to be distinct. We need L4 to be sunny. Could we simply take the line through (1,4) with slope 1? That would be y = x + 3, which is sunny. Does it cover any other points in P? (2,5) not in P. So it's fine. So we have 4 lines: three horizontal lines (y=1,2,3) and one sunny line (y=x+3). Are all lines distinct? Yes. But note: we have three non-sunny lines (y=1,2,3) and one sunny line (y=x+3). That's k=1. Does this cover all points? Check:

- y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)
- y=2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2)
- y=3 covers (1,3),(2,3)
- y=x+3 covers (1,4) (since 1+3=4) and also (2,5) not present. So all points covered. So k=1 works.

Similarly, k=2 for n=4? We attempted earlier and seemed impossible. But let's see if we can construct with 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines covering all points. We need to cover 10 points. With 2 non-sunny lines, max coverage (distinct) is 2*n = 8, but they may overlap. The remaining points (2) would need to be covered by 2 sunny lines. We have points:

1 (1,1)

Now suppose we take non-sunny lines: L1: y=1 (covers 1,5,8,10). L2: x=1 (covers 1,2,3,4). That's two non-sunny lines, covering all points with a=1 or b=1, except maybe some. That covers (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That's 7 points. The remaining points are (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 3 points. We have 2 sunny lines left. Could we cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines? We need a matching covering them. Let's examine pairs: (2,2)-(2,3) vertical, not sunny; (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal, not sunny; (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1, not sunny. So any line containing two of them is non-sunny. So with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered (if each sunny line covers at most one). But we have 3 points, so impossible. So that fails.

What about using non-sunny lines: L1: y=1, L2: y=2? That covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1) and (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). That's 7 points. Remaining: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) but (4,1) covered by y=1. So (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2) are 4 points. With 2 sunny lines, can we cover them? As before, we have sunny pairs (1,3)-(3,2) and (1,4)-(2,3) is not sunny, (1,4)-(3,2) is not sunny, (1,3)-(2,3) is horizontal, not sunny. So only one sunny pair, covering 2 points. The other two points (1,4) and (2,3) cannot be covered by a sunny line together. Could they be covered by separate sunny lines, but we have only 2 sunny lines total. So we could cover (1,3)-(3,2) with one sunny line, and then cover (1,4) with another sunny line (maybe also covering (2,2) already covered). But (1,4) and (2,2) are on a sunny line (y=-2x+6). That would cover (1,4) and (2,2). (2,2) is already covered by y=2. That's fine. Then (2,3) remains uncovered. Could (2,3) be covered by the same line (y=-2x+6)? That line passes through (2,2) and (1,4), not (2,3). So not. Could we cover (2,3) with a sunny line like y = x + 1? That covers (1,2) and (2,3). (1,2) is already covered by y=2. So that line would cover (2,3). But then we have 3 sunny lines? Actually we used one sunny line for (1,3)-(3,2), and another sunny line for (1,4)-(2,2). That's two sunny lines. To cover (2,3) as well, we would need a third sunny line. So not allowed.

Thus k=2 seems impossible. But we need a rigorous proof for all n≥3.

Now, we also have n=3, k=2 impossible. So the pattern is not simply k ≤ n-1, because for n=3, k=n-1=2 fails. So the necessary condition derived from coverage bound is not sufficient, and we need to determine exactly which k are possible.

Let's compute the bound from coverage inequality for odd n. n = 2m+1. Then M_n = (n+1)/2 = m+1. Then LHS = (n - k) * n + k * M_n = n^2 - nk + k (m+1) = n^2 + k (m+1 - n). Since n = 2m+1, m+1 - n = m+1 - (2m+1) = -m. So LHS = n^2 - k m. Also n(n+1)/2 = (2m+1)(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1? Let's compute: (2m+1)(2m+2)/2 = (2m+1)(m+1). That's (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1.

Now LHS = n^2 - k m = (2m+1)^2 - k m = 4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m.

We need LHS ≥ (2m+1)(m+1) = 2m^2 + 3m + 1.

So:

4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m ≥ 2m^2 + 3m + 1 → 4m^2 + 4m + 1 - k m - 2m^2 - 3m - 1 ≥ 0 → (2m^2 + m) - k m ≥ 0 → 2m^2 + m ≥ k m → if m > 0, divide by m: 2m + 1 ≥ k → k ≤ 2m + 1 = n.

So that bound gives k ≤ n. That's trivial. So for odd n, the bound from coverage does not restrict k (except that k ≤ n). So the necessary condition from coverage inequality is not restrictive for odd n. So we need a different approach to determine possible k for odd n. For n=3, we saw k=3 works. For n=5, we can attempt to construct configurations with many sunny lines. The maximum sunny lines possible is n, but we need to see if it's possible to cover all points with n sunny lines. For n=5, we need 5 sunny lines covering 15 points. Each sunny line can cover at most M_n = floor((n+1)/2) = 3. With 5 sunny lines, max coverage (distinct) is ≤ 5 * 3 = 15. So it's possible in terms of bound. However, we need to see if there exist 5 sunny lines covering all 15 points. That's like a covering problem. We can try to construct using lines of slope 1 and other slopes. For n=3, we used lines with slopes 1, -1/2, -2, which are all sunny. That's 3 lines. For n=5, we could try to use lines of slope 1 (y=x+c) for c = 0,1,2,3,4? That gives 5 lines, but they cover at most 3 points each (c=0 gives 3 points). The union of these 5 lines would be points with b - a = c. That's all points with b - a from 0 to 4, but many points with negative differences are missing. So not covering all points. We need a more clever design. Possibly we can use lines that are all "sunny" but also cover many points by choosing rational slopes with small denominators. For n odd, M_n = (n+1)/2, which is about n/2. With n lines, the total capacity is about n^2/2, which is exactly the total number of points. So if we can design a set of n sunny lines such that each point lies on at least one line, and the lines are distinct. This is reminiscent of covering a triangular grid with lines of certain slopes. Perhaps the answer is that k can be any integer from 0 to n inclusive, except some values? Let's test n=3, we found k=0,1,3 are possible, but k=2 is not. So not all. For n=4, we found k=0,3 are possible, k=1 also possible (we constructed). Did we construct k=2 for n=4? We attempted but seemed impossible. But maybe there is a construction with k=2 for n=4 that we haven't found. Let's try systematically.

For n=4, total points 10. We need 4 lines. Let's try to see if we can have 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can be chosen to cover many points. Define P_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines that can contain many lattice points are those with rational slope. The maximum number of lattice points in P_n on a line is something like ceil((n+1)/2) for slope 1, but also for slope 2? For slope 2, max is floor((n+1)/3). So less. So the maximum is indeed floor((n+1)/2) achieved by slope 1 (if n+1 is even? Actually for n=4, floor((5)/2)=2, slope 1 gives 2 points. For n=5, floor(6/2)=3, slope 1 gives 3 points. However, is it always achievable with a sunny line? Yes, slope 1 is sunny. So the maximum possible coverage by a sunny line is M_n. That is, there exists a sunny line (y = x) that covers M_n points. For n even, M_n = n/2. For n odd, M_n = (n+1)/2. So that's the maximum.

Now, to cover all points with n lines, we need to allocate coverage. The bound we derived using coverage capacity (n - k) * n + k * M_n ≥ total points is necessary, but not sufficient. However, we can also derive a necessary condition based on the number of lines that can cover points with a certain property. Another approach is to consider the points on the boundary or the lines with slope 0, ∞, -1. Since sunny lines cannot be parallel to those, maybe there is a combinatorial argument about covering the points with a+b = constant (anti-diagonal) lines. For example, the set of points with a+b = s (for s = 2..n+1) lie on lines parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1). Those lines are not sunny. So if we have many sunny lines, they cannot cover many of those anti-diagonal points because they are not on those lines. However, a sunny line can intersect an anti-diagonal in at most one point, perhaps, but could it contain two points from different anti-diagonals? Actually any line (unless it's parallel to x+y=0) can intersect each anti-diagonal line (x+y = constant) in at most one point. So if we have a sunny line, it can contain at most one point from each anti-diagonal (since if it contains two points with the same a+b, then they'd be on the same anti-diagonal line, which would be parallel to x+y=0, but a sunny line is not parallel to that, so it can intersect that line at most once). So indeed, for a sunny line L, for each s (2..n+1), |L ∩ P_n with a+b = s| ≤ 1. So a sunny line can cover at most one point from each anti-diagonal. The anti-diagonals have sizes (s-1) for s≥2. The total points is sum_{s=2}^{n+1} (s-1) = n(n+1)/2.

Now, we have n lines total, exactly k are sunny. The remaining n-k are non-sunny, which are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0. Those lines can cover many points from a single anti-diagonal (since a line parallel to x+y=0 is exactly an anti-diagonal line). So if we have many non-sunny lines, they can cover many points.

- L1: y = x + 1 (covers (1,2),(2,3) → (2,3) is in P2, (1,2) not)
- L2: y = -1/2 x + 3.5 (covers (1,3),(3,2) → (3,2) is in P2, (1,3) not)
- L3: y = x + 2 (covers (1,3),(2,4) → (2,4) is in P2, (1,3) not)
- L4: y = -2x + 6 (covers (1,4),(2,2) → (2,2) is in P2, (1,4) not)
- L5: y = -3x + 8 (covers (1,5),(2,2) etc) but (1,5) not in P2.

So we have many sunny pairs among P2. We need to cover all 6 points with 3 lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from P2 (as we argued), we need each of the 3 lines to cover 2 points, and the pairs must be disjoint (or at least union covers all). So we need a perfect matching covering the 6 points with 3 edges, each edge corresponds to a sunny line containing both points. So we need to partition the 6 points into 3 pairs, each pair collinear on a sunny line. Let's list all sunny pairs among those 6 points (points with a,b≥2, a+b≤6). The points are:

E (2,2)
F (2,3)
G (2,4)
H (3,2)
I (3,3)
J (4,2)

We need to find all pairs (p,q) such that the line through them is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Let's compute slopes:

- (2,2)-(2,3): vertical → not sunny.
- (2,2)-(3,2): horizontal → not sunny.
- (2,2)-(4,2): horizontal → not sunny.
- (2,2)-(3,3): slope 1/1 = 1 → sunny.
- (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal.
- (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal.
- (2,2)-(3,3) sunny.
- (2,2)-(2,4) vertical.
- (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal.
- (2,2)-(4,2) horizontal.
- (2,3)-(2,4): vertical.
- (2,3)-(3,2): slope (2-3)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny.
- (2,3)-(3,3): slope 0 → horizontal? Actually (2,3)-(3,3) is horizontal (y=3), not sunny.
- (2,3)-(4,2): slope (2-3)/(4-2) = -1/2 → sunny.
- (2,4)-(3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-2) = -2 → sunny.
- (2,4)-(3,3): slope (3-4)/(3-2) = -1 → not sunny.
- (2,4)-(4,2): slope (2-4)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1 → not sunny.
- (3,2)-(3,3): vertical → not sunny.
- (3,2)-(4,2): horizontal → not sunny.
- (3,3)-(4,2): slope (2-3)/(4-3) = -1 → not sunny.

So the sunny pairs are:

(2,2)-(3,3)
(2,3)-(4,2) (slope -1/2)
(2,4)-(3,2) (slope -2)

Also (2,2)-(3,3) is sunny. That's three pairs. Are they disjoint? The points involved are:

(2,2) and (3,3) -> covers (2,2),(3,3)
(2,3) and (4,2) -> covers (2,3),(4,2)
(2,4) and (3,2) -> covers (2,4),(3,2)

That's all six points! So indeed, we have a perfect matching. So we can take the three sunny lines:

L1: line through (2,2) and (3,3) → equation y = x (slope 1)
L2: line through (2,3) and (4,2) → equation y = -1/2 x + 7/2? Let's compute: (2,3) and (4,2). Slope = (2-3)/(4-2) = -1/2. Intercept: y = -1/2 x + b → 3 = -1 + b → b = 4. So line y = -1/2 x + 4. At x=4, y = -2+4=2, works. That's sunny.
L3: line through (2,4) and (3,2) → slope = (2-4)/(3-2) = -2. Intercept: y = -2x + b → 4 = -4 + b → b = 8. So line y = -2x + 8. At x=3, y = -6+8=2, works. That's sunny.

Thus with non-sunny lines H1 (y=1) and V1 (x=1), we have 2 non-sunny lines, and these 3 sunny lines cover the remaining 6 points. That's a total of 5 lines. The number of sunny lines is 3. So k=3 works for n=5. That's n-2? n=5, n-2=3. So k=3 works.

Now what about k=2 for n=5? We need to see if it's possible. With 2 sunny lines, we have 3 non-sunny lines. The total points are 15. If we can cover all points with 3 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. The 3 non-sunny lines could be, for example, H1 (b=1) covers 5, H2 (b=2) covers 4, H3 (b=3) covers 3 → total 12 points covered. The remaining 3 points (with b≥4) are (1,4),(2,4),(1,5). As we saw, no sunny line contains two of them. With 2 sunny lines, max coverage of those 3 points is 2 if we use one sunny line covering one point and the other covering another, leaving the third uncovered. But we could also use non-sunny lines that cover some of those points, but we already used 3 non-sunny lines. Could we choose a different set of 3 non-sunny lines that cover more points, leaving a triple that can be covered by 2 sunny lines?
